WGA Puts a Number on Studio Buying Power: 35% of the Market for Writing Services on Top-Grossing Films
· Deadline · Ted Johnson · 2026-07-22 · https://deadline.com/2026/07/writers-guild-paramount-warner-bros-lawsuit-1237000083/ · Provenance: filing/transcript source ↗
The Facts
- The Writers Guild of America filed a motion Wednesday morning in federal court in Oakland seeking a preliminary injunction to halt Paramount's acquisition of Warner Bros. Discovery. The guild's case is separate from the 12-state attorneys general suit.
- The WGA's core claim is a labor-market antitrust theory, not a consumer one: the transaction is "presumptively illegal in the market for writing services for anticipated top grossing films," where the combined company would hold a 35% share.
- The guild argues irreparable harm because a completed merger would be "virtually impossible to unwind," rendering any divestiture remedy ineffective, and cites "lost employment opportunities, suppressed compensation, and narrowed creative possibilities."
- On the balance of equities, the WGA argues any harm from a delayed closing is "self-inflicted" — a reference to Paramount's own deal terms, which carry a $7 million per day ticking fee for every day the merger is not closed past Sept. 30.
- The WGA is asking the court to align its hearing with the state AGs' Aug. 3 preliminary-injunction hearing. Paramount's attorneys "could not secure their clients' consent" to that schedule. Absent an expedited schedule, the WGA says it "would be forced" to seek its own TRO.
- Sequence set by the court: state AG PI motion due Thursday, Paramount opposition Monday, state reply July 30.
- Paramount's position, stated when the WGA suit was filed: a combined company "will have the scale and resources to reverse the current trends in our industry and expand opportunities for writers, not shrink them: more development slates, more series and film greenlights."
What This Actually Is
This is the first time in this merger fight that a guild has asserted itself as an antitrust plaintiff in the market for its members' own labor — and it has done so with a number attached. The 35% figure for "writing services for anticipated top grossing films" is a definition of a market, and a defined market with a share above the presumptive-illegality threshold is the single most useful artifact to come out of this litigation for anyone who sells creative services to studios. Whatever the court does with it, that number now exists in a federal filing.

The second move is tactical and worth reading closely. The WGA is not primarily trying to win on the merits this month; it is trying to buy time against a clock the buyer built for itself. Paramount's $7M/day ticking fee (roughly $650M per quarter) was structured to force speed. The WGA is turning that structure into an argument: you cannot claim urgency-based hardship from delay when you priced the delay into your own agreement. That is a sophisticated inversion — the deal's own financing architecture has become the plaintiff's equity argument.

Note what the guild did *not* do: it did not lead with content, consumers, or price. It led with employment, compensation, and creative range. Labor-market antitrust has been an ascendant theory in Washington for several years; this is its highest-profile application yet in entertainment.
Industry Read
1. [Company] A federal filing now carries a defined "market for writing services for anticipated top-grossing films" at 35% post-merger concentration. Concentration arguments against Paramount and Warner Bros. Discovery have historically been rhetorical from the sell side; this one has a docket number and a share figure, and it survives the motion regardless of outcome.
2. [Deal Type] The ticking fee is the pressure point, and both sides know it. At $7M/day the buyer's cost of delay compounds fast. Anyone with business pending at either company — a project awaiting a greenlight, an agreement awaiting signature, an option nearing lapse — should assume buyer urgency is real and treat it as leverage on timing terms specifically: short fuses, drop-dead dates, pay-or-play triggers.
3. [Talent Archetype: writer] The harms the filing names — lost employment opportunities, suppressed compensation, narrowed creative possibilities — are the writer-category consequences of buyer consolidation, stated in a court document rather than a bargaining position. That gives the archetype a citable articulation of its own market injury.
4. [Trend] Labor-market antitrust has arrived in entertainment at scale. If the court engages the market definition rather than dismissing it, buyer concentration becomes legally cognizable as a talent-side harm, and the theory is portable to every other creative-services category.
California's Next Incentive Fight Is About Post, Not Principal Photography
· The Hollywood Reporter · Katie Kilkenny · 2026-07-22 · https://www.hollywoodreporter.com/business/business-news/sean-astin-sag-aftra-postproduction-tax-credit-1236653309/ · Provenance: named-exec-quote source ↗
The Facts
- Gov. Gavin Newsom's final state budget, unveiled June 29, did not include funding for AB 2319, California's proposed postproduction tax credit. The bill survives and now must secure funding via a trailer bill.
- AB 2319, carried by Assemblymember Nick Schultz, would offer a 35 percent base tax credit to productions that locate postproduction work in California but shoot elsewhere, or that shoot in California without receiving a production credit. Under current law, post expenses qualify only if principal photography also occurs in-state.
- SAG-AFTRA's president released a video to THR ahead of a California Post Alliance (CAPA) town hall held Tuesday night on the Sony lot, urging members to lobby their representatives. This is the union's first top-officer intervention; SAG-AFTRA had filed a written letter of support earlier in the month.
- The union's stated ask: the $750 million production tax credit was a win, but "now we have to incentivize companies to keep postproduction work in California."
- Existing coalition backing the bill: CAPA (sponsor), the Editors Guild (co-sponsor since June), L.A. Mayor Karen Bass, the California IATSE Council, Netflix and Apple.
- The bill has passed the state Assembly and enters the Senate Appropriations Committee on August 3, in what THR characterizes as a cash-strapped budget year.
What This Actually Is
The 2025 expansion of California's production credit to $750M solved for stages and shooting days. It did not solve for the work that happens after wrap, and post is the easiest part of the pipeline to move, because it does not require the physical presence of cast, locations, or crew. A colorist, mixer, editor, VFX house, or ADR loop group can sit anywhere with bandwidth. That means post is where incentive arbitrage bites hardest and where the marginal decision is cheapest for a studio to make.

AB 2319's design is the interesting part: it deliberately decouples post from principal photography. It offers 35% to a production that shoots in Georgia, Hungary, or Australia and finishes in Los Angeles. That is not a "keep the whole show here" bill — it is an admission that California has lost the shooting-day fight in a meaningful share of cases and is now competing for the segment it can still win. It is defensive industrial policy, and it is honest about it.

The politics are the constraint, not the merits. The governor left it out of the budget in a tight year, so it goes to a trailer bill — a harder, later, more contingent path. That is why the advocacy escalated to the union presidency this week: the coalition needs volume, not new arguments. Worth noting who is already in the coalition. Netflix and Apple, two of the most location-agnostic buyers in the business, are publicly supporting a California-specific incentive. Their post pipelines are concentrated here even when their shoots are not.
Industry Read
1. [Talent Archetype: actor] The constituency in play is performer labor that lives in post — singers, voiceover artists and loop groups. Voice and ADR work is the actor-side labor most exposed to offshoring, and increasingly the labor most exposed to synthetic substitution. An incentive that anchors that work in California functions as a jobs floor for a performer category whose leverage is otherwise thin.
2. [Deal Type] If AB 2319 passes, post location becomes a negotiable point rather than a studio default. A 35% credit is large enough to change where a production finishes, which moves "where does post happen" from an administrative afterthought to a term with money attached — and one that affects whether a creative team stays employed locally between projects.
3. [Company] Netflix and Apple backing a state incentive is a read on their own cost structure. Two buyers with no structural need for California are lobbying for California post, which says their post infrastructure is here and sticky. That is useful whenever either company argues a project's finishing costs are immovable.
4. [Trend] August 3 is the date, and it is the same day as the Paramount-WBD injunction hearing. Senate Appropriations is where cash-strapped-year bills die quietly. If it clears, the trailer-bill funding fight is next; if it stalls, expect post migration out of the state to continue and the coalition to reload for 2027.
IATSE Organizes a $90M Warner Bros. Animated Tentpole in Louisiana, 84-35
· The Hollywood Reporter · THR staff · 2026-07-22 · https://www.hollywoodreporter.com/business/business-news/workers-dynamic-duo-studio-swaybox-unionize-1236653489/ · Provenance: named-exec-quote source ↗
The Facts
- Creatives at Swaybox Studios, a New Orleans-based studio producing Dynamic Duo for Warner Bros. Pictures Animation and DC Studios, voted to join IATSE in an election concluding Tuesday. The tally: 84 for, 35 against.
- Absent valid challenges, a unit of 137 workers — animators, digital puppetry artists, CG modelers and others — joins the union.
- IATSE says the film is budgeted at $90 million and calls the campaign "one of the largest organizing drives in film and television production undertaken by IATSE in recent years."
- *Dynamic Duo* is being made with Swaybox's proprietary "Momo animation" technique — a blend of CGI, stop-motion and live-action performance using puppets.
- IATSE Local 478 business agent Simonette Berry: workers sought "a voice on the job," union-standard wages and benefits, and changes to working conditions, adding that "productions coming to Louisiana to build world-class films can't expect to succeed by lowering standards."
- IATSE international VP Mike Miller called on Swaybox and Warner Bros. to "come to an industry standard agreement immediately."
- Swaybox was founded in 2014; its prior credits of note are an NFL commercial and a canceled DC project. THR sought comment from Swaybox and WB Pictures Animation.
What This Actually Is
Three separate structural facts collide in this one vote, and the combination is the story.

One: a major studio put a nine-figure DC tentpole into a non-union shop in a tax-credit state whose prior credit of note was a commercial. That is the runaway-production economics of the last decade, applied not to live-action shooting days but to the animation pipeline itself. Two: the technique — puppets plus CGI plus live-action performance — sits in a craft-jurisdiction gray zone, neither cleanly traditional animation nor cleanly VFX, which is exactly the kind of novel workflow that historically escapes existing agreements. Three: the workers organized anyway, at scale, with a 70% yes margin, and IATSE immediately named the studio, not just the vendor, as the party it expects at the table.

That last move matters most. The standard studio position on a vendor shop is that the labor relationship belongs to the vendor. IATSE's public demand that "Swaybox and Warner Bros." reach an industry-standard agreement is a direct challenge to that separation. The economics of using a low-cost regional vendor for a $90M studio picture only work if the studio's cost basis stays outside the union framework; if organizing follows the work into the vendor, the arbitrage compresses.
Industry Read
1. [Trend] Animation's cost arbitrage now has an organizing counterweight, and it moved fast. A studio routing work to a regional non-union shop can no longer assume the cost basis holds for the life of the project. For anyone valuing an animated production — a budget, a bond, a backend model — the labor line is less predictable than it was last week.
2. [Deal Type] "Momo animation" is the useful precedent, not the IP. Hybrid puppet/CGI/live-performance workflows are proliferating because they are cheaper and jurisdictionally ambiguous. This vote establishes that ambiguity is not protection — and the classification question is identical for AI-assisted pipelines, which are the next place it gets tested.
3. [Company] IATSE naming the studio alongside the vendor is the term to watch. If Warner Bros. engages, vendor-shop insulation weakens across the business and "we don't control our vendor's labor terms" becomes a harder answer to give. If it does not engage, watch whether the union escalates to the picture itself.
4. [Talent Archetype: producer] For producers and production companies working through regional vendors, a vendor's union status is now a live variable over the life of a production, not a fixed input at quote. Anyone signing a production-services agreement in a right-to-work jurisdiction should price the possibility that the shop organizes mid-schedule.
News Corp Countersues Over the Middleman: Scraping and Reselling Content to AI Companies
· Reuters (Jonathan Stempel), via The Star · 2026-07-22 (filed July 21) · https://www.thestar.com.my/tech/tech-news/2026/07/22/news-corp-countersues-brave-for-allegedly-039scraping039-articles-for-ai- · Provenance: filing/transcript source ↗
The Facts
- News Corp filed a countersuit Tuesday in federal court in Oakland against Brave Software, accusing it of "flagrant theft" for distributing and selling versions of Wall Street Journal and New York Post articles to AI companies.
- News Corp says Brave's "covert scraping" and resale fall "nowhere near the bounds" of fair use. It seeks an injunction, unspecified monetary damages, and statutory damages of up to $150,000 per infringement.
- The core economic claim, quoted from the filing: "The more content Brave copies and sells, the more revenue it generates, and the less incentive AI companies have to negotiate licenses with the publishers who produced the content. Brave profits while publishers are cut out."
- Procedural posture is inverted: Brave sued first, in March 2025, seeking a declaratory judgment that bundling licensable copyrighted articles is not infringement. It filed a revised complaint in May 2026 after what News Corp calls failed negotiations for a "fair, market-based agreement."
- Brave's defense: indexing to make content searchable, and serving users snippets and "high-level summaries," is fair use. Brave has also argued News Corp is threatening to disrupt generative AI advances.
- News Corp's chief executive said Brave's conduct shows "blatant disregard," adding: "This era of tacky tech trafficking must come to an end if journalism is to have a sustainable future."
- The New York Post, Dow Jones, and News Corp's British and Australian operations are also defendants in Brave's suit.
What This Actually Is
Most AI copyright litigation to date pits a rights holder against the model developer — the party that trained on the work. This case is about the layer in between: an aggregator that scrapes, packages, and resells copyrighted material into the AI supply chain. That is a different and arguably more consequential target, because the middleman is what makes it possible for a model company to acquire content without ever negotiating with the owner.

Read the economic argument in the filing carefully, because it is the part that generalizes: the harm alleged is not merely that copying occurred, but that the existence of a cheap unlicensed channel destroys the market for the licensed one. If a model developer can buy a bundle from a third party, it has no reason to sign a direct license. That is a market-foreclosure theory dressed in copyright, and it is the same theory any rights owner would use against any intermediary that packages their work for AI ingestion.

Note also that Brave moved first, and defensively — seeking a declaratory judgment that bundling is lawful, and doing so after receiving a cease-and-desist. That sequencing tells you the intermediary layer knows its position is contested and would rather pick its forum than wait.

The read-across to film and television is direct. Studio and talent-side content sits in exactly the same posture: it can be scraped, bundled, and resold by parties who never had a relationship with the rights holder, and the licensing market for that content is still being formed. Whether the reseller-in-the-middle is liable is a question that gets answered once and then applies to everyone.
Industry Read
1. [Trend] The middleman theory is what to track, not the headline. If a court holds that scraping-and-reselling to AI companies is not fair use, liability attaches to every aggregator in the chain — giving rights holders a defendant far easier to identify and enjoin than a foreign model developer. That materially improves the enforcement position of anyone who owns or controls audiovisual IP.
2. [Talent Archetype: actor] The market-foreclosure argument is directly portable to likeness, voice, and performance data. "An unlicensed channel destroys the incentive to license" is precisely the argument for a performer whose voice or image is being packaged by a third-party dataset vendor — and there is now a template complaint with a live federal docket behind it.
3. [Deal Type] Statutory damages at up to $150,000 per infringement are the leverage, because per-work arithmetic scales catastrophically against a defendant holding a large corpus. Registration discipline is what unlocks that number, which makes copyright registration of a library a commercial decision rather than a clerical one.
4. [Company] Failed licensing negotiations preceded the litigation. That is the emerging pattern across the AI-content market: negotiate, fail, litigate, and let the ruling set the price. Rights holders waiting for a market rate to emerge by consensus should expect it to be set instead by whoever litigates first.
BAFTA Gives Itself Audit Power Over AI Use — Without Banning It
· Variety · Alex Ritman · 2026-07-22 · https://variety.com/2026/film/awards/bafta-tweaks-film-awards-rules-updates-ai-guidance-1236817925/ · Provenance: named-exec-quote source ↗
The Facts
- BAFTA published eligibility and voting changes for the 2027 film awards, characterized by Variety as small tweaks relative to AMPAS's recent Oscar changes.
- The AI language. Having added "human achievement" to the rulebook last year, BAFTA has now added: the awards recognize "outstanding human achievement in originating, shaping and realising the final artistic outcome, regardless of the tools used to bring this work to life. Where the use of Generative AI or other digital tools is relevant to the consideration of the work, BAFTA reserves the right to request additional information regarding the nature of such use and the extent of human creative involvement."
- BAFTA's executive director of awards and content said the addition gives BAFTA the "opportunity to just dig in a bit deeper if there's something we want to check," with the film committee ruling on eligibility. Asked whether a fully AI-generated film or performance could ever be nominated: it "would always be a human that picks up a BAFTA."
- Outstanding British Film: longlist expands from 15 to 20 after round one; nominations stay at 10 (five by membership vote, five by jury).
- Performance categories: BAFTA's casting chapter will now vote alongside the acting chapter in longlisting and nominations, to "increase the expertise" across 700+ candidates in four categories.
- BAFTA is not following the Oscars in permitting multiple nominations for one performer in the same category — previously allowed, banned after a review, and retained as banned to "level the playing field."
- Bullying and harassment: CIISA-alignment declarations remain mandatory for British categories; BAFTA now requests the information from all submitted productions, with a stated expectation it becomes a requirement in future years.
- Key dates: entries open July 22; round one voting Dec. 4; longlists Jan. 7; nominations open Jan. 19; final voting Jan. 26; ceremony Feb. 21.
What This Actually Is
BAFTA chose disclosure-on-demand over prohibition, and that is the whole substance of the change. There is no AI ban, no percentage threshold, no mandatory declaration at entry. Instead the academy has written itself a discretionary audit right: if something looks off, it can compel a production to document its AI use and the extent of human creative involvement, and the film committee decides. "Regardless of the tools used" is an explicit safe harbor for AI as an instrument; "human achievement in originating, shaping and realising" is the line the tool cannot cross.

This is a defensible design and a deliberately soft one. It avoids the definitional swamp — nobody has to decide in advance whether a de-aged face, a synthetic crowd, an AI-assisted temp mix, or a generative previs disqualifies a film. It also avoids policing the ninety-nine percent of productions where AI use is mundane. The cost is that the standard is now case-by-case and opaque: nobody will know where the line is until a specific film is asked to explain itself, and that inquiry will happen privately.

The other change worth noticing is structurally unrelated but points the same way. Casting directors get a vote in performance categories. That is a small governance move with a real consequence: the people who assemble performances now help judge them. In a season where "what counts as a performance" is the live question, adding casting expertise to the performance electorate is not incidental.
Industry Read
1. [Trend] Awards bodies are converging on disclosure rather than prohibition, and the disclosure is demanded of the production, not the vendor. Any film using generative tools should assume it may have to reconstruct, after the fact, who did what with which tool. That is a records problem before it is a rules problem — productions that cannot document human contribution will struggle regardless of what they actually did.
2. [Deal Type] "Human achievement in originating, shaping and realising the final artistic outcome" is usable contract language: a clean, awards-tested articulation of the human-authorship line, drafted by a body with no stake in either side's deal position. It belongs in the vocabulary for any AI provision being negotiated.
3. [Talent Archetype: high-end filmmaker] The audit right creates a new diligence item for anyone whose film carries awards value in its business plan. AI-use provenance becomes a value-protection question, since eligibility now depends on documentation a production may not have kept.
4. [Company] For distributors running dual UK/US campaigns, the two bodies have diverged: BAFTA keeps the ban on double nominations in a category while AMPAS has lifted it, and the British Film longlist widens to 20. Campaign strategy is no longer symmetrical across the two.
A 100-Title Microdrama Slate: Vertical Video Buys Volume the Way Studios Once Bought Output
· Deadline (EXCLUSIVE) · Jesse Whittock · 2026-07-22 · https://deadline.com/2026/07/buzzfeed-studios-muvpix-microdrama-romeo-juliet-reimagined-1237000057/ · Provenance: named-exec-quote source ↗
The Facts
- BuzzFeed Studios and vertical-video firm MuVpix have partnered to produce more than 100 titles over the next two years, launched with a cross-platform distribution strategy.
- First title: Romeo & Juliet, Reimagined, launching in August, transplanting the Montague-Capulet feud into the modern-day Los Angeles criminal underworld. Shot in both horizontal and vertical formats.
- MuVpix launched in March 2026 as the vertical entertainment platform of BlackForge Distribution, with two originals (*Whispervale*, *Swipe Left*), adding *Romeo & Juliet, Reimagined* in June.
- The stated model: co-create original IP, reimagined classics, and "shoppable" formats built "natively for TikTok, Instagram Reels, YouTube Shorts, FAST/CTV platforms and the muVpix Originals platform."
- BuzzFeed contributes vertical storytelling operations, production infrastructure, marketing and "cultural reach"; MuVpix contributes platform, distribution and tech.
- MuVpix's founder: the partnership brings "premium storytelling to a format that audiences are already showing up for in massive numbers… at a scale and quality level the space hasn't seen before."
- Corporate context: the pact comes ~two months after Byron Allen closed a deal to acquire BuzzFeed. The plan is to split BuzzFeed Studios and Tasty into independent entities, remaining wholly-owned subsidiaries.
- BuzzFeed Studios is also attached to conventional features. Deadline calls this its biggest microdrama play to date.
What This Actually Is
Strip the format novelty and this is an output deal, structured at a scale legacy television abandoned. One hundred titles in twenty-four months is roughly a title a week, sustained — a volume commitment no scripted studio has made in a decade. The economics that permit it are the point: microdrama unit costs are low enough that the slate, not the title, is the risk unit. You are not greenlighting a show; you are funding a portfolio and letting distribution sort it.

Two details signal that this is not purely a social-native experiment. First, the launch title was shot in both horizontal and vertical — meaning the asset is built from the start to travel to CTV and FAST, not just to a phone feed. That is a hedge against the format itself, and it implies a real production budget rather than a creator-grade one. Second, the distribution list explicitly names FAST/CTV alongside TikTok, Reels and Shorts. The ambition is to supply the ad-supported television ecosystem with content produced at vertical-video cost.

"Shoppable formats" is the third leg, and the most consequential one. It means the revenue model is not only advertising and platform rev-share but commerce integration — the model creators already run, and one legacy scripted has never successfully attached to narrative.

Note the timing against ownership. This lands two months after the BuzzFeed acquisition closed, with a stated plan to spin Studios into a standalone entity. A 100-title slate is the kind of asset that makes a subsidiary look like a business worth separating.
Industry Read
1. [Deal Type] Volume is re-entering the market, just not where scripted talent is currently pointed. A hundred greenlights over two years are being committed in a format with no guild framework, no residual structure, and no established credit norms. That is simultaneously an employment opportunity and an unpriced category — someone will set the terms in this lane, and it should not be the platforms by default.
2. [Talent Archetype: emerging filmmaker] A title-a-week cadence at low unit cost is one of the few remaining volume training grounds for writers, directors and performers early in a career. The relevant question for that archetype is not whether to work in the format but what to retain: credit, clip rights, and the ability to carry the work forward as a sample and as IP.
3. [Trend] The dual-format shoot is the detail that makes this credible. Producing horizontally and vertically from one shoot means these assets are intended to compete for CTV and FAST inventory — the same ad dollars as licensed library television, which is where a great deal of back-end value currently sits.
4. [Deal Type] "Shoppable" is the term to understand before it appears in an agreement. Commerce-integrated narrative creates a revenue stream traditional participation definitions do not capture. Define commerce revenue into the participation base explicitly, or it will be treated as ancillary platform revenue and disappear.
A Broadway Musical Grossing $1M a Week Is Now a Money-Loser — and Only Five New Ones Are Set for Next Year
· The Hollywood Reporter (July 22 magazine issue) · Caitlin Huston · 2026-07-22 · https://www.hollywoodreporter.com/business/business-news/cats-broadway-musicals-1236653875/ · Provenance: named-exec-quote source ↗
The Facts
- Cats: The Jellicle Ball — the critically acclaimed revival restaging the musical as a queer ballroom competition — announced an Aug. 8 closing after roughly five months on Broadway. It won three Tony Awards but missed Best Revival.
- Grosses were hovering around $1 million a week pre-Tonys, with high attendance. The company was told at a July 13 meeting that the show could not generate enough revenue to cover expenses.
- THR's reporting locates the problem not in attendance but in average ticket price: the show could fill seats, but not at prices sufficient to cover elevated Broadway operating costs.
- Capitalization comps in the piece: *Cats: The Jellicle Ball* up to $18M (described as relatively low for this era); *Hadestown* $11.5M, recouped, now in year seven; *Paddington*, a technical show arriving in spring, capitalized up to $31M.
- Only five new musicals have been announced for next year.
- Cost drivers cited: post-pandemic supply-chain and materials inflation, utilities, theater rent, and rising rates for actors, musicians, stagehands and creative teams. Musicals are hit hardest — larger casts plus an orchestra — while plays increasingly carry celebrities who can command higher prices.
- A lead producer on a long-running Tony-winning musical, quoted: "I'm watching a lot of shows not quite find the audience that can sustain them… There have always been more shows that fail than succeed. I think the proportion right now feels distorted."
- The show's composer wrote publicly on July 14: "Without action, Broadway risks rivaling Hollywood's empty soundstages: increasingly dark theatres where bold new work once lived," calling on theater owners, producers and union leaders to convene.
- Producer responses so far are individual, not collective: fewer set pieces, less automation, smaller casts, longer marketing runways, discounted previews. One incoming musical's lead producer: "The amount of time that you have for your show to pick up has shortened."
- On audience conditioning: "We've trained audiences that the shows of value are the ones with stars and recognizable IP, and so now we have to try to dig ourselves out of that behavior pattern because that's not sustainable either."
What This Actually Is
This is a cost-base failure, not a demand failure, and the distinction is the entire read. Attendance was fine. Reviews were strong. It won Tonys. It closed because the price the audience would pay did not clear the price of putting the show on. When a $1M-a-week musical loses money, the break-even has moved above what the market will bear for anything without a star or a pre-sold title — and that is precisely what the five-new-musicals-for-next-year number is measuring. Supply is contracting because the risk has become unpriceable.

The second-order effect is the one that reaches film and television. Audiences have been trained that value equals stars plus recognizable IP. That is the same conditioning described in theatrical exhibition for a decade, arriving on Broadway with the same consequence — original work becomes the residual category, and the two things that reliably open a show are a name and a title people already know.

There is a sharp irony sitting one story over. The same week this revival closes for want of ticket revenue, a live capture of another Tony-winning musical is opening in 1,944 theaters with roughly $5M in advance sales and a $7M–$10M opening outlook (per Deadline) — a single weekend approaching a meaningful share of what a Broadway musical grosses in two months. The stage economics are failing while the captured version of the same art form finds a cheaper, wider distribution path. That is not a coincidence; it is a migration.
Industry Read
1. [Trend] The stage-to-screen supply pipeline is narrowing at the source. Five new musicals announced for next year means five fewer proven-on-their-feet properties entering the adaptation funnel each cycle. For buyers who treat Broadway as an IP farm system, the input is thinning — which raises the price of the properties that work and rewards whoever already holds one.
2. [Talent Archetype: movie star] Star-and-IP conditioning now governs two markets at once. A screen star's ability to open a Broadway play is a distinct, priceable asset, because celebrity-carried plays are the productions clearing the cost base. That is a real and growing revenue lane, denominated in a market where alternative supply is collapsing.
3. [Deal Type] Live capture is the arbitrage, and it is working right now. A capture opening in 1,944 theaters against $5M in presales proves a stage property can reach a national theatrical audience at a fraction of a touring or sit-down cost base. Capture rights should be treated as a separately negotiated, increasingly valuable right — not a documentary afterthought bundled into a stage agreement.
4. [Company] Nobody has proposed a collective solution, so the cost base keeps rising. The public call for owners, producers and unions to convene has produced individual budget-trimming, not structural change. Absent that, expect continued contraction in new musicals, continued reliance on stars and known titles, and continued migration of the form toward captured and screen distribution.
"The Balance of Power Has Shifted": A Directors Ranking Whose Premise Is the Story
· Vulture / New York Magazine · Nate Jones · 2026-07-21 · https://www.vulture.com/article/100-most-powerful-directors-in-hollywood.html · Provenance: analyst-inference source ↗
The Facts
- Vulture published The 100 Most Powerful Directors in Hollywood, a year in the making, with an explicit thesis in the deck: "10 years ago, franchise producers dominated the movies. Now, the buzziest deals belong to auteurs."
- The framing argument: the industry is exiting the cinematic-universe era, when mega-producers "ruled the land," and "directors seem to matter again."
- Cause identified: a generation of cinephiles "raised on YouTube, Reddit, and Letterboxd" who came of age under MCU hegemony and now "bristle at interconnected comic-book worlds the same way their forebearers in the '70s did at old-fashioned musicals."
- Talent-side evidence cited: the multi-year role preparation of a marquee young screen star went to an auteur project, "not a superhero."
- A leading industry analyst, quoted directly: "The balance of power has shifted." Previously a "big-time commercial director" was considered far more important than any Oscar-friendly auteur. "But because of the rise of film-fan culture, the communities built around filmmakers, that has given more power to the auteurs who can deliver an audience."
- The implicit benchmark for the top of the list: "the person whose latest movie started selling tickets a year ahead of time."
What This Actually Is
The ranking is the packaging; the premise is the asset. Vulture is asserting — with a leading analyst on the record — that the unit of commercial trust in theatrical has moved from the producer-custodian of a universe to the director as a brand that opens a film. That is a claim about where the audience's purchase decision now originates, and if it is right it reprices a whole category of deal.

The stated mechanism is worth taking seriously because it is structural rather than sentimental. Film-fan culture built on Letterboxd, Reddit and YouTube gives a filmmaker a direct, measurable, portable audience relationship — the thing that made stars bankable in an earlier era, now attached to the person behind the camera. A director with that relationship carries their audience across studios, genres and budget tiers, which is exactly what a franchise cannot do once the universe fatigues. And the demand signal is legible in advance: tickets selling a year out is a pre-release audience proof no comparable-title analysis produces.

Two cautions. First, this is a magazine feature and a critic's thesis, not data — the provenance is analyst inference. Second, the market can hold both propositions at once: this week's box office is led by an auteur event picture and the following weekend belongs to a superhero tentpole projected to open larger. "Auteurs matter again" is not "franchises stopped working." What is genuinely new is that the auteur lane is now underwritable at event scale, which was not true in 2016.

Still, the counting matters. When a list of a hundred names is compiled and the industry argues about the order, the category itself has been ratified.
Industry Read
1. [Talent Archetype: high-end director] The pre-sale window is emerging as a bankability proof, and it is a number. Tickets moving a year ahead of release is a measurable demand signal that exists before a single comp is available. A director whose last picture generated early on-sale demand has a quantitative argument for first-dollar participation, format guarantees and marketing commitments that does not depend on the cast attached.
2. [Deal Type] A director-brand audience is portable in a way a franchise seat is not. The custodial-producer role derives value from the IP; the filmmaker relationship travels with the person. That asymmetry is the argument for retained rights, sequel and derivative participation, and control terms — the buyer is renting an audience that leaves when the filmmaker does.
3. [Talent Archetype: movie star] Actor-side leverage is increasingly routed through directors. The scarce commodity a screen star now spends years on is a filmmaker relationship, not a franchise slot. That reorders how packages assemble and whose attachment moves a greenlight — the director attachment increasingly secures the cast, not the reverse.
4. [Trend] Treat this as a consensus artifact, not evidence. It is a critic's thesis with one on-the-record analyst quote. Its value is that it becomes the frame the town argues inside — useful in a room, dangerous if mistaken for a market study. Build a talking point on it; do not build a valuation on it.
Paramount Wins Brussels and Asks for a Mini-Trial as the $7M-a-Day Clock Runs
· Deadline + Paramount Skydance investor release + Variety, The Wrap, The Verge (corroborators) · 2026-07-22 · https://deadline.com/2026/07/paramount-warner-bros-state-attorney-general-lawsuit-1237000888/ · Provenance: filing/transcript source ↗
The Facts
| | |
|---|---|
| EU clearance | European Commission formally cleared Paramount Skydance's acquisition of Warner Bros. Discovery on July 22, with conditions — including Paramount giving up its stake in United International Pictures within 13 months of closing (per the Commission's decision, via The Verge) |
| Jurisdictions cleared | Paramount's release counts 65 jurisdictions that have cleared or declined to challenge, incl. US DOJ, Australia, Brazil, Canada, China (SAMR), South Korea, South Africa, plus foreign-direct-investment clearances in Germany, France, Spain, Italy and others |
| US posture | A federal judge issued a 14-day temporary restraining order on Monday (7/20) and set an Aug. 3 hearing on a preliminary injunction, in the 12-state attorneys-general antitrust suit |
| Paramount's ask (filed 7/22) | A three-day evidentiary hearing — effectively a mini-trial — the week of Aug. 17 or Aug. 24, plus consent to extend the TRO through the court's decision |
| The money | After Sept. 30, Paramount owes WBD a $7 million per day ticking fee if the deal has not closed. Paramount's brief says an injunction would cost it "well over $1 billion in ticking fees and other additional costs" |
| Competing schedules | Court's schedule: state opening brief Thursday, company response Monday, state reply July 30. Paramount proposed: openings July 28, company reply Aug. 7, state response Aug. 12 |
| WGA | Filed its own preliminary-injunction motion, seeking to align with the states on Aug. 3; Paramount called that "not feasible" but workable later in August |

Paramount's release argues the Commission's findings "directly refute" the state complaint's market definition — specifically that the Commission declined to treat high-budget/"blockbuster" films as a separate relevant market, and defined film production to include "smaller US studios such as Amazon MGM, A24 and Lionsgate, as well as European studios."
What This Actually Is
Two clocks are now running against each other, and the second one is the story. Brussels removed the last major *regulatory* obstacle; what remains is a domestic *litigation* obstacle with a price tag attached. The ticking fee converts delay itself into a transfer of value from Paramount to WBD at $7M/day, which means Paramount's legal strategy is no longer primarily about winning — it is about compressing the calendar. That is exactly what a three-day evidentiary hearing in mid-August buys: the appearance of full process at a speed that keeps the deal inside the Sept. 30 window.

The state plaintiffs understand this. Their counsel already dismissed an earlier acceleration bid as "two experts in a food fight in a month." A preliminary injunction that runs eight months does not need to defeat the merger on the merits to be decisive; at $7M/day it is an economic weapon. Paramount's willingness to consent to an extended TRO is the tell — it will trade a longer freeze for a faster ruling, because the freeze is cheap relative to a PI.

The EU decision also hands Paramount a rhetorical asset it is already deploying: an independent regulator, on a full record, refusing to define "blockbuster films" as its own market. That is the exact factual terrain the state complaint stands on.
Industry Read
1. [Company] Every deal negotiated with either company between now and Sept. 30 is being priced against a $7M/day clock. A counterparty burning that much per day of delay is a counterparty with an unusual reason to close things — not just the merger, but everything adjacent that touches the integration story. Speed is available; the cost of asking for it is lower than usual.
2. [Deal Type] The ticking fee is the term to steal. Most talent-side deals put no price on the buyer's delay. This is a clean, public precedent for a per-day charge that starts on a date certain and makes the *other* side's slippage expensive — worth writing into any deal where a buyer's stall costs real optionality (a holding period, an exclusivity window, a start date).
3. [Trend] Watch the market-definition ruling, not the outcome. If a US court adopts the Commission's read — that Amazon MGM, A24 and Lionsgate are genuine competitors to a merged Paramount-WBD, and blockbusters are not a distinct market — that reasoning becomes the template for the next round of consolidation, and it materially widens what any two studios can do together.
4. [Company] The UIP divestiture is the underpriced detail. International distribution infrastructure coming loose is a structural change in how a merged entity reaches non-US theatrical, and it should be read into any international-rights or output-deal conversation with either company over the next 13 months.
A Filmmaker-Owned AI Studio Just Raised $11M of a $15M Round — Outside the Studio System
· The Hollywood Reporter (EXCLUSIVE) · corroborated by Deadline and Variety · 2026-07-22 · https://www.hollywoodreporter.com/business/business-news/darren-aronofsky-raising-money-ai-film-studio-1236654262/ · Provenance: filing/transcript source ↗
The Facts
| | |
|---|---|
| The entity | An AI film and content studio founded in 2025 by an Oscar-nominated feature director with a producing partner, both listed as executive officers |
| The raise | An SEC filing earlier in July 2026 for a $15 million equity raise; the document states just over $11 million already sold, first sale July 1 |
| Use of funds | Not stated in the filing. The company's site lists open roles for "generative artists," social media strategists, a chief of staff, and a "Head of Studio" who will "oversee the development, production and commercialization of our slate, including digital-first creator content, feature films, television series, and branded content" |
| Model partnership | A non-exclusive filmmaking partnership with Google DeepMind, under which the company produced a 45-minute featurette (first of a planned trilogy) that premiered at Tribeca in 2025 |
| Prior output | A set of American Revolution-themed shorts using AI generations of historical figures, premiered winter 2025-26 to significant public backlash; pivoted last month to an animated approach |
| Comparison set | THR notes other established filmmakers using AI for storyboarding and animated development, and studios including A24 and Lionsgate having forged creative partnerships with AI companies |
What This Actually Is
This is the first clean example of a top-tier filmmaker capitalizing an AI production company on the equity market rather than through a studio partnership — and the corporate shape says more than the technology does. A $15M raise with a Head of Studio hire whose remit spans "digital-first creator content, feature films, television series, and branded content" is not an experiment. It is a vertically integrated content company being stood up with venture-style money, where the founder's name and taste are the asset and AI is the cost structure that makes a four-format slate affordable at that check size.

The model partnership being explicitly non-exclusive is the load-bearing detail. The filmmaker keeps model optionality; the model provider gets a credible creative showcase without owning the output. That is a materially better position than the studio-side AI partnerships (A24, Lionsgate), where the studio is the counterparty and the filmmaker is a participant in someone else's deal.

The backlash on the earlier shorts is worth holding alongside this, not against it. The reputational cost was real and the work was publicly pilloried, and the response was to raise money and hire a studio head. That is what conviction looks like when it is financed.
Industry Read
1. [Deal Type] A director-owned AI studio raising outside equity is a new financing archetype worth having a term position on before a client asks. The unsettled questions: who owns the models' outputs, what happens to the equity if the founder's name comes off the company, whether talent appearing in AI-generated work has a separate license, and whether the model provider gets any downstream participation. A non-exclusive model partnership plus a founder-controlled cap table is the structure to argue for.
2. [Talent Archetype: high-end filmmaker] The capital-raise route reprices what a filmmaker's production company can be. A traditional overall deal buys a filmmaker's time in exchange for a studio's infrastructure; an equity raise buys the infrastructure outright and keeps the upside. At $15M — small by studio standards, real by venture standards — this is now inside reach for any filmmaker with a name and a thesis, which changes the alternative any studio is bidding against.
3. [Company] For studios, the competitive threat is not that AI films get made; it is that the filmmakers they want are becoming their own counterparties, financed by capital that does not need a theatrical window to return. Expect studios to respond with more aggressive first-look terms on the *company*, not just the person.
4. [Trend] The $11M-of-$15M-already-sold detail says investor appetite is not the constraint. Watch whether the next such raise is larger, and whether any institutional media money (rather than tech venture money) shows up on a cap table — that is the tell for whether this becomes a category or stays a one-off.
YouTube Books an $11B Ad Quarter and 1.7B World Cup Viewers — the Scale Number Every Streamer Is Now Measured Against
· The Hollywood Reporter + Deadline, on Alphabet's Q2 2026 results and prepared CEO remarks · 2026-07-22 · https://www.hollywoodreporter.com/business/business-news/youtube-ad-revenue-reported-giant-alphabet-google-profits-1236654342/ · Provenance: filing/transcript source ↗
The Facts
| | |
|---|---|
| YouTube ad revenue, Q2 2026 | More than $11 billion, up 13% year over year |
| Alphabet total revenue | $119.8B, up 24% YoY |
| Google advertising (Search + YouTube + Network) | $81.6B, of which Search alone was $63.3B |
| Google Cloud | Roughly $25B, growing 82% |
| Subscriptions | $12.9B |
| EPS | $9.11 — nearly quadrupled; beat consensus |
| World Cup | Over 1.7 billion unique viewers watched World Cup-related videos on YouTube during FIFA World Cup 2026, per Alphabet's prepared remarks |
| Capex guidance | Full-year 2026 capital expenditure raised to $195–205B from $180–190B (per The Information) |

*Verification note: THR's copy states Alphabet's "Q2 profit beyond $112 billion." That figure is not consistent with the $119.8B total-revenue and $9.11 EPS figures in the same paragraph and is almost certainly a transcription error for a revenue line. It is not carried as a fact here.*
What This Actually Is
YouTube's advertising line alone, at $11B a quarter, is running at roughly $44B annualized — larger than the total revenue of most of the companies talent negotiates with, and growing 13% while doing it. This is not a new fact so much as a newly unavoidable one, and the World Cup number is what makes it concrete: 1.7 billion unique viewers on YouTube for event coverage, in a quarter when the same tournament anchored every rights-holder's schedule. YouTube did not buy the World Cup. It monetized the conversation around it at a scale no rights holder matched.

The strategic content is in the ratio. Alphabet is guiding to $195–205B of capex, and YouTube's ad business is a rounding error against Search. That means YouTube's creator economics are set by a parent optimizing for something other than YouTube's P&L, and it can sustain revenue-share terms and creator payouts that a studio funding content off its own content budget structurally cannot match.
Industry Read
1. [Company] Every streamer's creator strategy is now being executed against a competitor whose ad business grew 13% to $11B in a quarter and does not need that business to carry the company. When a streamer offers a creator a licensing deal, the comparison is not "streamer vs. no deal" — it is "streamer's guarantee vs. an uncapped platform share." Price accordingly.
2. [Deal Type] The right structure for creator-side deals against this backdrop is non-exclusive licensing with retained channel ownership, not an all-rights buyout. Keeping the channel keeps an asset compounding at platform growth rates; giving it up in an exclusivity trades a growing annuity for a fixed guarantee. That trade needs to be priced explicitly, not assumed away.
3. [Talent Archetype: producer] The 1.7B World Cup figure is a live-event data point, not a scripted one, and that distinction matters. Platform scale converts most cleanly on event, reaction and companion content — the formats a producer can stand up quickly around a rights window someone else paid for. That is a real, addressable business adjacent to any major sports or event property.
4. [Trend] Watch whether YouTube's ad growth rate holds above the streamers' total revenue growth for another two quarters. If it does, "engagement share" stops being a narrative device in earnings calls and starts being the metric that sets talent compensation ceilings across the market.
The New Development Path: Make It for $1,000, Post It to Instagram, Then Take the Meeting
· The Ankler — Like & Subscribe · 2026-07-22 · https://theankler.com/instragram-scripted-tv-boom-is-just-getting-started-outpacing-youtube/ · Provenance: reported-named-sources source ↗
The Facts
| | |
|---|---|
| The case study | Two writers stuck in TV development made a 12-episode buddy comedy for $1,000 total, on a camera one of them already owned, and posted it to Instagram. ~1.5M views in under three months; brands have approached about funding a second season |
| Scale of the trend | A writer-producer tracking vertically-shot scripted comedies puts the count "above two dozen" and says "the space is accelerating rapidly" |
| The financed model | A production company run by a former head of comedy and drama at a major streamer won a social-first series away from a major studio by fully financing a six-episode first season with no distributor attached. It was made cheaply enough that direct distribution on YouTube and social remains a viable fallback that could still recoup |
| Benchmark cited | That series' pilot: 260,000+ YouTube views. Comparison from a streaming launch: a well-known comedy original averaged 800,000 viewers for its first five episodes after 11 days (per a third-party analysis) |
| Why Instagram over YouTube | Recommendation algorithms plus short-form focus — cheaper to produce than the long-form volume YouTube's watch-time economics demand. Only a handful of creators do scripted at scale on YouTube |
| Format constraint | The writers reconfigured their scripts because the Instagram algorithm favors videos under three minutes |
| Entry bar | "You don't want to start a show with less than 100,000 followers," per one comedy creator |
| The monetized version | A three-year-old Instagram workplace comedy: 530,000+ followers, weekly episodes, ~6 crew plus ~6 in post, entirely funded by brand deals. Its owner-creator has declined inbound Hollywood offers: "I own [the show] … To give that up, it would have to be the right opportunity" |
| Rep-side read | The co-CEO of a digital management firm: "A lot of people are trying to figure out how they can de-risk content spend by leaning into built-in audience" |
What This Actually Is
This is a functioning parallel development system, and the important thing about it is not that the shows are cheap — it is that the audience is now the pitch document. One writer's line is the whole mechanic: "When we have producer meetings now, we can say, 'Look how many people have already watched this.'" A greenlight conversation that used to be an argument about whether an audience exists is now an argument about how large a known audience is. That is a different negotiation with different leverage.

Three distinct business models are visible inside one trend, and they should not be blurred. Model one is proof-of-concept: make it for nothing, use the numbers to sell a half-hour. Model two is the fully financed indie season — an experienced buyer-side executive funding a season with no distributor attached, which is the indie-film model finally working in television because the production floor fell far enough. Model three is the one Hollywood is least prepared for: a fully brand-funded show whose owner has no particular reason to sell, employs a dozen people, and treats a studio offer as an option rather than an outcome.

The choice of Instagram over YouTube is a cost-structure fact, not a taste one. YouTube's watch-time economics require length and frequency that cost real money; Instagram's algorithm rewards a sub-three-minute episode, which is what $1,000 buys. Format is following financing, and creators are rewriting scripts to fit the distribution math. That is exactly the direction of causality worth watching.
Industry Read
1. [Talent Archetype: writer] For any writer sitting on a project that will not clear a buyer's development gate, self-produced social distribution is now a rational, cheap, reputation-safe move rather than a consolation prize. The material cost is near zero, the downside is bounded, and the upside is a measurable audience that converts directly into buyer meetings. This belongs on the standard options list.
2. [Deal Type] When a buyer does come calling on one of these, the transaction is an acquisition of a working asset with an existing revenue line, not a development deal — and it should be papered that way: retained IP where possible, a separate valuation for the social channel and its brand-partner relationships, and a carve-out so the creator can keep running the social version. The refusal above is the leverage template; the buyer needs the audience more than the creator needs the buyer.
3. [Company] "De-risk content spend by leaning into built-in audience" is now an articulated strategy from the rep side, which means built-in audience will be priced into offers within a year. The window in which a creator converts audience into a premium is open now and will narrow as this becomes standard diligence.
4. [Talent Archetype: emerging filmmaker] The fully-financed-with-no-distributor model is the one to track hardest. If it recoups even once publicly, the number of series reaching the market without a buyer's approval goes up sharply — and the buyer's traditional gatekeeping leverage goes down with it.
A Federal Production Incentive Now Has Republican Sponsors and a Studio CEO Lobbying for It
· The Hollywood Reporter — EXCLUSIVE, July 22 magazine issue · 2026-07-22 08:40 · https://www.hollywoodreporter.com/business/business-news/republicans-hollywoods-federal-tax-incentive-push-1236650473/ · Provenance: reported-named-sources source ↗
The Facts
| | |
|---|---|
| The mechanism | A stackable federal incentive layered on top of existing state credits for film and TV shot in the U.S., to compete with Australia and Canada, which already do this |
| The lobbying | Paramount Skydance has spent $2.86 million on lobbying so far in 2026 (House disclosures), covering "issues related to federal film/TV production incentives" among other areas; meetings with Senate, House, FCC and Treasury staff |
| The visit | Paramount Skydance's chief executive went to D.C. July 13 to court Republican members of a key House committee — the same day the states filed to block the $111B Paramount-WBD merger |
| Republican backers named | Rep. Brian Jack (GA — district includes Trilith Studios) as a key backer alongside longtime Democratic sponsor Rep. Laura Friedman (CA); also Reps. Nicole Malliotakis (NY), Nathaniel Moran (TX), Mike Carey (OH), Kevin Hern (OK) |
| On the record | Malliotakis: "There is a growing coalition of actors, of labor unions, of film production companies and a bipartisan group of members that want to see something happen by the end of the year." Hern cites Oklahoma's industry having "created over 22,000 jobs since 2021" |
| The coalition | Hollywood's top unions and studios, the administration's special ambassador to the industry, and independent filmmakers. An economic impact study is close to publication; a D.C. "day of advocacy" is under discussion |
| The blocker | The executive director of the Coalition for American Production: "Members of Congress are waiting for the president's OK to go ahead and sponsor this. Meanwhile, the White House is waiting for them to propose a vehicle, so it's a little bit of a standoff" |
| Vehicle | Undecided — a tax package, the next reconciliation package, or a standalone bill. Target committee: House Ways and Means |
| Senate | Less clear. Sen. Adam Schiff still pushing; Paramount's CEO is said to have met some Democratic senators about a bipartisan approach |
| The skepticism | Labor sources doubted the CEO's impact and called him "a controversial messenger" mid-merger, while conceding his White House relationship "can't hurt" |
What This Actually Is
The consequential shift here is not the sponsor list, it is the substitution of an incentive for a tariff. The administration's prior enthusiasm ran to punitive tariffs on foreign-made projects; a stackable domestic credit is the opposite instrument, and a coalition that includes both the unions and the studios has now organized around it. As one source puts it: "In this Washington, it just takes the White House to make it a priority to make it happen."

The standoff described is a specific, resolvable kind — not opposition, but a coordination failure between members waiting for presidential cover and a White House waiting for a legislative vehicle. That means the probability of passage is less a function of political support than of who moves first, which makes the timeline genuinely unpredictable and the trigger genuinely watchable.

The studio CEO's position is the interesting complication. He is simultaneously the most motivated advocate (a merged Paramount-WBD would be among the largest beneficiaries) and the most compromised messenger (asking Washington for help while defending an antitrust suit). Labor's read — skeptical of his impact but unwilling to refuse the help — is the accurate one.
Industry Read
1. [Trend] A stackable federal credit would move the location economics of every U.S.-shot production, which flows directly into budgets, schedules and above-the-line room. Where the current answer is "we can't afford it unless we shoot in Australia," the answer changes. Any long-lead production planning through 2027 should carry this as a live scenario, not a hypothetical.
2. [Deal Type] Incentives are negotiable value. When a federal credit exists, who captures it — studio, financier, or the production entity a client controls — becomes a real deal point, exactly as state credits already are in independent financing. Get ahead of the default that it all accrues to the studio; a producer or filmmaker with a financing entity has a legitimate claim to share in a benefit their U.S.-shoot decision creates.
3. [Company] Paramount's $2.86M lobbying spend, disclosed mid-merger, signals how the post-merger company intends to operate: policy is a channel it will use. Read that into any expectation about its behavior on regulatory-adjacent matters.
4. [Talent Archetype: producer] The near-term actionable item is the economic impact study now close to publication. It will be the coalition's core evidence, and it is a free, credible dataset on U.S. production employment and runaway-production losses — useful for any argument about where and why to shoot domestically, independent of whether the legislation passes.
A Hot Package Got Passed On Because of a Franchise Conflict, Not the Material — and Landed at a Rival
· Deadline — EXCLUSIVE · 2026-07-22 12:30 · https://deadline.com/2026/07/sony-ridley-scott-treasure-island-hugh-jackman-1237000487/ · Provenance: trade-rumor (sources tell Deadline; Sony declined comment) source ↗
The Facts
| | |
|---|---|
| The package | A pirate-genre feature adaptation of a public-domain 19th-century adventure novel, with a top-tier feature director attached and a movie star in the lead villain role, from a produced screenplay by a well-regarded adapter |
| Where it landed | Sony Pictures is in negotiations to acquire it. Sony declined comment |
| The first look | 20th Century — the director's usual studio home, releasing his next film in August — got first look and passed |
| Why it passed | Not the material and not the relationship: 20th is a Disney division, and Pirates of the Caribbean is a priority at Disney's live-action unit, so Disney did not want a competing pirate project and let it go to market |
| Why Sony won | Sony "was high on it from the jump," which Deadline ties to the director's long-standing relationship with Sony's studio head — who championed the same director's films while co-chairman of 20th Century, and backed two of his more recent pictures |
| Timeline | Deadline first reported the package last month, after the director declared it his next project |
| Underlying IP | The source novel (1883) has sold 100M+ copies in 50+ languages; a prior animated adaptation was released by Disney in 2002 |
What This Actually Is
The reported reason for the pass is the whole story, and it is unusually clean: a top-tier director's home studio declined a package it liked, from a filmmaker it wants to keep, because the parent company is protecting an adjacent franchise. That is portfolio conflict, not a quality or relationship judgment — and it is a mappable, predictable constraint that most packaging conversations treat as noise.

The corollary matters more than the transaction. A studio's slate is a set of occupied lanes, and a project that lands in an occupied lane is competing against a sibling division's priority before anyone reads a page. That risk goes up, not down, as the majors consolidate: the more franchises sit under one roof, the more lanes are pre-occupied, and the more often a "no" is really "not here."

Sony winning on relationship is the second half of the mechanic. A distributor without a competing franchise slate and without a streamer to feed has structurally fewer conflicts and can move on an executive relationship. That arm's-length position, usually described as Sony's weakness, is here the reason it is holding the package.
Industry Read
1. [Deal Type] Diagnose a "no" before accepting it. A pass driven by franchise adjacency is not information about the package's value — it is information about that buyer's slate. The practical move is to map the conflicting lane before the first-look conversation, and where a home-studio pass is foreseeable, negotiate the first-look waiver early and go to market with momentum rather than after a public rejection.
2. [Company] For consolidating buyers, the number of pre-occupied lanes is rising. Any project touching a franchise a major already owns should be assumed conflicted at that major *and its subsidiaries*, and taken to a buyer whose slate is clear. That is a direct, near-term argument for the value of distributors without competing franchise portfolios.
3. [Talent Archetype: high-end director] A director's "go-to studio" relationship is a real asset and a real ceiling. It got the package first look; it did not get the package made. The durable relationship here is with the executive, not the corporate entity — the same studio head backed the same filmmaker across two different companies. That is the relationship worth investing in, and worth tracking as executives move.
4. [Talent Archetype: movie star] A star-plus-auteur package on public-domain source material remains a saleable unit at a major in the current market, with multiple studios taking a look. That is a live data point for what a star attachment is still worth on an original-adjacent theatrical film, against a market narrative that says only owned IP moves.
A Franchise Its Creators Lost Two Decades Ago Came Back — and Is Now Monetizing a Second Window
· Variety / Daily Variety podcast · 2026-07-22 · https://variety.com/2026/film/news/scary-movie-directors-cut-michael-tiddes-pvod-daily-variety-1236817286/ · Provenance: named-exec-quote (the film's director on the record) source ↗
The Facts
| | |
|---|---|
| The film | *Scary Movie*, a Paramount and Miramax release |
| The window | The extended director's cut bowed on PVOD platforms July 22 |
| The extras | 13 minutes longer than the theatrical cut, with all-new scenes not in theaters, plus over 30 minutes of bonus features, including a previously unseen alternate ending |
| The rights history | The franchise's original creators lost the rights to the property in the early 2000s amid a dispute with the then-head of Miramax, and have since "rightfully gotten it back" |
| On the record | The director: "This movie was a fantastic full circle … the [creators] had lost this franchise a number of years ago and now rightfully gotten it back. So for that, I was extremely happy because *Scary Movie* was back with the original guys who created the franchise, where it belongs" |
| On the extras | "There's over 30 minutes of bonus features and an extended cut that is 13 minutes longer than the theatrical cut, and it features all new scenes not seen in theaters. So there's more jokes, more chaos, and even more lines crossed" |
What This Actually Is
Two mechanics are stacked in one small item, and the second one is the durable lesson.

The surface story is a PVOD second-window product strategy: not a port of the theatrical cut, but a distinct 13-minutes-longer edit with an alternate ending and half an hour of bonus material — a deliberate reason to pay for a film that already played. This is the DVD-extras playbook rebuilt for a digital window, and it treats the PVOD release as its own product with its own value proposition rather than as the theatrical run's exhaust.

The real story underneath is reversion. The creators lost a franchise in a rights dispute in the early 2000s, recovered it, and it is now generating a theatrical release plus a monetized second window under their control, roughly a quarter century later. Franchise IP does not decay on a human timescale. A property taken from its creators — or signed away in a weak-leverage moment — can be worth a full production cycle decades later, and reversion is the term that determines who gets that cycle.
Industry Read
1. [Deal Type] Reversion is the most underpriced term on the sheet, and this is the argument to make with it. Its value is not measured against the current deal's economics; it is measured against what the property is worth on the next cycle, which may be twenty-plus years out. Push for time-based reversion, an unexploited-rights reversion trigger, and a right of first negotiation on any revival — especially on deals where leverage is weakest at signing, which is exactly when these clauses get traded away.
2. [Deal Type] PVOD deserves a separate definition, not a residual one. If a distributor can build a materially different product for the digital window — extended cut, alternate ending, 30+ minutes of new material — then the participation definition, the bonus features' authorship, and compensation for producing that additional cut all need to be specified. A "home video" or "digital" catch-all written for a straight port undervalues a window where the distributor is making a distinct product.
3. [Talent Archetype: producer] The second-window product is also a work-for-hire question: someone conceived, shot and cut 13 minutes of new material and 30 minutes of extras. Whether that work sits inside the original directing services, is separately compensated, or is credit-bearing is a live negotiating point every time a distributor plans a distinct PVOD edit.
4. [Company] For Paramount and Miramax this is a template worth watching: a legacy comedy franchise, revived with its original creators, extended into a second window with a differentiated product. If the PVOD number is good, expect the extended-cut-as-second-window play on more mid-budget titles — a monetization change that touches every participation definition in the category.
