YouTube Analog-Horror Becomes Theatrical's Newest IP Engine: 'Mandela Catalogue' Lands After 11-Studio War, 'Obsession' Hits $403M
· Deadline (Anthony D'Alessandro, Justin Kroll) · 2026-07-02 (deal) + 2026-07-05 (Obsession milestone) · https://deadline.com/2026/07/youtube-mandela-catalog-steven-spielberg-scott-stuber-1236973259/ · Provenance: reported-named-sources (Deadline exclusive; named companies + reported box office actuals) source ↗
The Facts
- The Mandela Catalogue — a viral YouTube analog-horror series (100M+ views, launched 2021) — has been won for the big screen after an 11-studio bidding war by United Artists, Amblin and Amazon MGM Studios. The series' creator directs from a script he co-wrote. Paper Street Pictures also produces.
- The deal rides a proven wave: Obsession (Focus/Universal, a YouTube horror-romance) is at $403M WW (8th weekend); Backrooms is at $331M+ WW. Both outgrossed anticipated tentpoles including a Star Wars title and DC's Supergirl.
- The stated strategic driver: these titles convert the historically hard-to-reach Gen Z / Gen Alpha / under-30 audience into theatrical ticket buyers.
What This Actually Is
Hollywood has found a new, cheap, pre-validated IP class: creator-owned YouTube horror franchises with built-in Gen Z/Alpha audiences and micro-budget origins. Two adaptations have already out-earned studio tentpoles, and an 11-studio bidding war for the next one shows the majors now treat this pipeline as a core acquisition target, not a novelty. The economics are the point: a fandom-tested horror concept with a young built-in audience, adaptable at a fraction of tentpole cost, is exactly the risk-adjusted bet a market burned by $170M superhero bombs wants.
Industry Read
1. [Trend] A new IP source class has been validated at scale: YouTube-native genre franchises out-grossing studio tentpoles. The 11-studio bidding intensity means acquisition competition — and price — for the next viral horror property is now real. This resets where "hot IP" is sourced.
2. [Talent Archetype: emerging filmmaker] The creators of these franchises are going from YouTube to directing their own studio features — a fast, repeatable path from platform-native creator to feature director. A talent pipeline to get ahead of, and a comp for what a proven audience is worth in a first feature deal.
3. [Deal Type] The structural question on every one of these is rights and backend for the creator-owner: they arrive owning a franchise with a measurable audience — maximum leverage on ownership retention, sequel/franchise participation, and directing attachments, very different from a spec sale.
4. [Trend] Micro-budget horror remains the highest-margin theatrical bet, now supercharged by pre-built fandoms. The genre-health signal is strongly positive and the Gen Z/Alpha theatrical audience is finally being reached — repricing young-skewing genre material upward.
Crunchyroll Founder Launches AniBiz, A Global B2B Anime-Licensing Marketplace
· Variety (Jennifer Maas) · 2026-07-02 · https://variety.com/2026/tv/news/crunchyroll-founder-new-anime-website-anibiz-1236800890/ · Provenance: named-exec-quote (company announcement + founder statements) source ↗
The Facts
- Crunchyroll founder/former CEO Kun Gao's new company Nakama launched AniBiz.com, billed as "the first dedicated B2B marketplace built specifically for the global anime industry" — a centralized platform to streamline anime licensing across rights holders, distributors, merchandisers, retailers, and global buyers.
- Launch licensors include Aniplex of America, Toei Animation, TOHO Global, Avex, Pony Canyon, TV Tokyo, TV Asahi, TBS and ~20 more top Japanese IP holders.
- Framing: the global anime market is estimated to exceed $35B this year, with IP demand outrunning the "traditional, largely fragmented" licensing system. AniBiz promises "data-driven insights into market trends and territory-specific demand."
What This Actually Is
This is infrastructure being built for anime as an asset class. The pitch isn't a new streamer — it's a rights-clearing layer that lowers the barrier to acquiring and collaborating on Japanese anime IP globally, at the moment demand has outgrown the fragmented, relationship-driven Japanese licensing system. When someone builds a marketplace and data layer for a category, it's because the category's transaction volume now justifies the tooling. Anime IP is being industrialized for cross-border dealmaking.
Industry Read
1. [Trend] Anime is being treated as a $35B IP asset class with dedicated dealmaking infrastructure — a structural signal that Japanese IP acquisition/adaptation volume is scaling and that sourcing friction is being deliberately removed. Anime IP competition will intensify.
2. [Company] A neutral, multi-licensor marketplace slightly disintermediates the platform gatekeepers (Sony/Crunchyroll, Netflix) that currently sit between Japanese rights holders and global buyers — a shift in who controls access to the pipeline.
3. [Deal Type] A centralized marketplace with territory-demand data changes the information asymmetry in anime rights deals — buyers and producers gain comps and demand signals they didn't have, a modest leverage equalizer on licensing terms.
4. [Talent Archetype: producer] For producers chasing adaptation rights to Japanese IP, a verified marketplace lowers sourcing friction and widens the field of accessible titles — while also widening the buyer pool competing for the same properties.

Orbital Studios Anchors at Television City, Betting Virtual Production Can Keep Runaway Shoots in Los Angeles
· Deadline · Variety · 2026-07-06 · https://deadline.com/2026/07/orbital-studios-moving-to-television-city-los-angeles-1236975338/ · Provenance: named-exec-quote source ↗
The Facts
- Virtual production company Orbital Studios (est. 2020) is setting up a brick-and-mortar home at Television City in Los Angeles.
- Orbital provides art teams, tools, software and pipelines to make virtual production "faster, leaner and easier to budget" while pushing final-pixel quality.
- Both trades frame the move as a play against runaway production: "enabling productions to capture global locations without leaving Los Angeles" to keep crew jobs and spend local. Orbital's CEO cites "the latest in virtual production technology and the most talented virtual art and AI artists."
- Television City is mid-way through a billion-dollar, city-approved expansion.
What This Actually Is
Infrastructure catching up to an economic problem. High-end production has been leaving California for cheaper incentive jurisdictions; virtual production (LED volumes plus real-time art pipelines) is the technical bet that you can shoot "anywhere" without physically going there. Orbital planting a flag inside a historic, expanding LA lot is the supply side of that bet, and its explicit pairing of virtual art with "AI artists" marks where the cost-compression pitch is heading next.
Industry Read
1. [Trend] Virtual production is being positioned as the answer to runaway production and the LA cost problem: build the global-location capability into an LA stage so the spend stays local. Anyone advising on where a project physically shoots now has a stronger in-town option to weigh against out-of-state incentive math.
2. [Trend] The "virtual art plus AI artists" framing is the leading edge of production-cost compression. As real-time and generative tools fold into the volume pipeline, the budget line for locations, sets, and VFX shifts, and so does the leverage in below-the-line and vendor negotiations. This is the AI-in-production cost curve showing up in physical infrastructure, not just software.
3. [Talent Archetype: high-end filmmaker] For filmmakers whose work depends on scale and world-building, in-town volume capacity changes the calculus on what can be delivered on a given budget and schedule, and strengthens the hand of directors who want to keep a production in Los Angeles without surrendering ambition.

· Deadline · 2026-07-06 · https://deadline.com/2026/07/emmys-tv-academy-rights-deal-2027-simulcast-1236972826/ · Provenance: trade-rumor source ↗
The Facts
- Primetime Emmys rights expire after the 2026 event (Sept 14, on NBC). The Television Academy is in talks over where the show airs from 2027 onward.
- Options: single network/platform; the traditional four-network "wheel"; a larger wheel including streamers; or a newly floated simulcast across multiple broadcasters and streamers sharing rights and production cost.
- Rights value is "thought to be around $10M per year" (current deal "just over $8M per year").
- Networks are cautious because the telecast "is no longer a major revenue generator"; streamers including Netflix have downplayed solo-ownership interest. Notably, YouTube took the Oscars from Disney's ABC beginning 2029.
What This Actually Is
The slow repricing of live awards telecasts, made visible. An ~$8M-to-$10M annual rights fee for a marquee ceremony, plus networks openly calling it "no longer a major revenue generator," marks how much the linear awards-show franchise has deflated. The genuinely new structural fact underneath it is the destination: YouTube, not a legacy network or a traditional streamer, is the entity that just captured the single biggest awards property. The Emmys' scramble is the same story one rung down.
Industry Read
1. [Trend] Awards-telecast rights are repricing downward and fragmenting (wheel, larger wheel, simulcast) because the linear ad model behind them has thinned. The "prestige of the statue" is decoupling from "the value of broadcasting the ceremony," which reframes how much leverage an awards platform actually confers versus the awards themselves.
2. [Company] YouTube taking the Oscars (from 2029) is the load-bearing signal: the premier live-event awards franchise migrated to a Google-owned platform, not a studio streamer. It extends YouTube's move from discovery layer to holder of premium live tentpole events.
3. [Trend] For talent, the calculus shifts from "which network carries my category" toward "the statue matters, the telecast home increasingly does not." That weakens any single distributor's ability to use awards-telecast association as leverage, and changes where campaign and visibility spend should be aimed.

Behind the 'Supergirl' Bomb: Competing Cuts, Creative Differences
· The Hollywood Reporter · 2026-07-03 · https://www.hollywoodreporter.com/movies/movie-features/supergirl-making-box-office-bomb-1236636425/ · Provenance: reported-named-sources source ↗
The Facts
- In March, the studio ran a formal bakeoff between two competing cuts of Supergirl — one from the film's director, one assembled by the studio's leadership — after test scores stalled in the 60s across four screenings and never cleared 70. The studio's cut won by only two points; the director's version tested better on song choice, pacing, and the villain, but was 11 minutes longer.
- Reported creative differences between the studio's leadership and the director led to an unusually hands-on post-production process: the studio brought in a favored writer and its own regular editor to help retool the film. The film was never tested again after the bakeoff.
- Context: the film (budgeted $170-180M) opened to $37.1M, below even a comparable prior comic-book bomb. A studio leader made a rare on-record comment defending the broader slate strategy despite the opening-weekend result.
What This Actually Is
This goes past "the movie flopped" into "the studio already knew" — a formal bakeoff run specifically because internal confidence had collapsed, with the eventual theatrical cut winning a test-score contest by two points, not on conviction. That is a structural tell about how a filmmaker-led studio leadership model is being run: creative calls are being made deep into post-production on projects the leadership isn't directing, then defended publicly when one goes wrong.
Industry Read
1. [Company] A demonstrated process failure — testing in the 60s for months without escalating until a forced bakeoff — is a live signal for any filmmaker evaluating a deal with a studio run by a filmmaker-executive: expect creative authority to extend deep into post-production even on projects that executive doesn't direct, and expect that authority to be exercised late and abruptly rather than early and collaboratively.
2. [Talent Archetype: high-end director] A director attaching to a studio under this kind of filmmaker-led leadership model should structure post-production and final-cut expectations explicitly going in — a competitively-testing cut still lost the bakeoff here, a cautionary precedent on how much a director's cut counts once the studio decides to intervene.
3. [Trend] Commentary from an unnamed studio leader that "Gen Z does not care about superhero movies" — paired with the same weekend's evidence that YouTube-native horror franchises are outgrossing legacy comic-book tentpoles — corroborates a genuine under-30 audience migration away from legacy IP toward creator-native genre content, not a one-studio problem.

'Minions & Monsters' Comes Up Short: Franchise-Low Domestic Open Despite Best-Ever Reviews
· Deadline (Anthony D'Alessandro) · 2026-07-03 · https://deadline.com/2026/07/box-office-minions-monsters-1236972754/ source ↗
The Facts
| Field | Value |
|-------|-------|
| Title | Minions & Monsters (Universal; dir./voiced Pierre Coffin) |
| Domestic 5-day est. | $63.5M (later nudged to ~$65M) vs. $80M tracking / $60M–$90M exhibitor range |
| Domestic 3-day | $38.5M at 4,243 theaters — No. 1 |
| Franchise context | Lowest 3-day AND 5-day opening in the Despicable Me/Minions franchise (7th film, $5.6B cume) |
| Prior July-4 opens | Despicable Me 2 $143M (2013, 5-day), Despicable Me 4 $122.6M (2024), Rise of Gru $123M (2022, 4-day) |
| Reviews / exit | 90% RT certified fresh (best-reviewed in franchise); A- CinemaScore (2nd for franchise); RelishMix social reach 793M, +31% vs. comps |
| International | $37.6M overseas through Thu; $62.6M global cume after 2 days; No. 1 in Germany, Spain, Poland, Netherlands; opened China at ~$4.6M day 1 (No.1); expanding to ~71 territories this weekend |
| Cost | $85M net, before global P&A |

The franchise historically pulls 60%+ of its global gross from overseas; two prior entries (2015 Minions, 2017 Despicable Me 3) crossed $1B, three more came close. Competitive frame this weekend: Toy Story 5 (Disney/Pixar) 3rd wk ~$30M/$365M cume; Young Washington (Angel Studios) $16–17M ahead of tracking; Supergirl (WB/DC) 2nd wk $10M, -73%; Disclosure Day (Universal) 4th wk $5.6M/$104.8M cume.
What This Actually Is
The most reliable animation engine in the business just posted the softest opening in its 16-year history — and did it with the franchise's best reviews and a strong A- exit. This is not a quality miss; it is a demand-ceiling signal. The seventh trip to the well, on the same Wednesday-into-July-4 launch pad that delivered $122M–$143M twice before, is tracking ~20% below its own $80M hope and roughly half of what the last two Independence Day entries opened to. The offsetting variable is international: the model is built to earn abroad, the overseas rollout is only half-deployed, and China opened No. 1. An $85M net negative cost means the film is not in trouble on the ledger — but the domestic number is the datapoint that matters for what the franchise is worth going forward.
Industry Read
1. [Company] Universal takes a clean No. 1 and a manageable loss, but the July-4 animation slot just repriced: the "guaranteed $120M+" assumption behind the last two Independence Day entries is gone. Expect a harder lean on the international window and P&A discipline, and the next animation dating decision treated as a real choice rather than a default holiday lock.
2. [Trend] Best-reviewed-ever + A- exit + record social reach + a franchise-low open is the clearest "brand fatigue beats marketing" datapoint of the year in family animation. Sequel-count is now a demand drag even for the strongest animation brand outside Disney/Pixar — resetting the comp math on any animated-sequel greenlight and strengthening the case for original animation.
3. [Talent Archetype: producer / production company] For the capital-efficient animation model, the read is that pricing power sits in the *system* (low budgets, studio output, global reach), not any single title — and a franchise-low domestic open argues for feeding that system fresh IP and licensed-game franchises rather than extending an aging core line at a rising cost basis.

As Paramount-Warner Merger Enters Antitrust Crunch Time In Europe, Britain Holds Key To The September Deadline
· Deadline (Jake Kanter) · 2026-07-02 · https://deadline.com/2026/07/paramount-warner-merger-antitrust-crunch-time-europe-1236973099/ source ↗
The Facts
- Paramount's ~$110B acquisition of Warner Bros. Discovery has cleared the U.S. DOJ and is nearing EU clearance after Paramount submitted concessions on June 30; the European Commission decides by July 22 whether to clear or open a phase-two probe.
- The UK is now the primary roadblock. The culture secretary is "minded to intervene," calling the deal "unprecedented." Paramount has until July 6 to make final representations; a formal intervention notice (the likely outcome) would trigger Ofcom + CMA reviews (40 days), then a decision to clear or refer to a phase-two probe of 5+ months.
- Paramount targets an end-September close, driven by a "ticking fee" to WBD shareholders of ~$650M per quarter the deal slips past Q3.
- EU concessions reportedly include Paramount exiting its UIP international-distribution joint venture with Universal and divesting some kids' TV networks. Combined UK weekly TV share: 12.1% (third behind BBC, ITV); 6.9% including streaming (behind Netflix and YouTube).
What This Actually Is
The deal has moved from "will it happen" to "when, and at what delay cost." US and EU are effectively de-risked; the UK is a timing risk, not an existential one — no serious observer expects a veto. The load-bearing number is the ticking fee: every quarter past Q3 transfers ~$650M from Paramount to WBD holders, so the UK's power is to push the close toward 2027, not to stop the combination. The UIP breakup is the industry-level tell — two majors' overseas theatrical distribution machinery is being dismantled to clear Europe.
Industry Read
1. [Company] Completion is now near-certain; the live variable is the close date and who absorbs the ~$650M/quarter delay cost. A combined "WarnerMount" is close enough to price into any position touching either company — but the entity may not legally merge until early 2027 in the worst case, so counterparties are still dealing with two separate companies whose leverage is shaped by the pending merger.
2. [Deal Type] The ticking-fee mechanic — a large, quantified per-quarter penalty for regulatory delay — is a clean precedent to file for any big media transaction with a long regulatory tail.
3. [Trend] The UIP JV breakup resets international theatrical distribution for two majors at once; any project leaning on a specific studio's overseas distribution muscle should assume that machinery is in flux.

What This Actually Is

The deliberate no-backend structure (an unknown lead at $400K) lowered breakeven but also reads as a studio hedging a title it wasn't fully convinced could open.
Industry Read

2. [Deal Type] The reusable point: studios building interconnected-universe entries around unproven leads will hold first-dollar-gross tightly and cast cheap to protect breakeven. That defines where leverage sits (and doesn't) for talent entering a shared-universe title as a newcomer vs. as an established star.
3. [Trend] Two 2026 flops against a strong 2025 reframes this studio's theatrical form as volatile, not broken — but shared-universe brand equity is not automatically transferring to lesser characters, a warning for the whole cinematic-universe model.

'Project Hail Mary' Arriving On Prime Video After 105-Day Theatrical Window
· Deadline (Anthony D'Alessandro) · 2026-07-01 · https://deadline.com/2026/07/project-hail-mary-prime-video-release-date-1236972341/ source ↗
The Facts
- Amazon MGM Studios' Project Hail Mary premieres on Prime Video July 3 — a 105-day theatrical-to-streamer window. It hit the studio's pay-one service (MGM+) first on June 18, a 90-day window.
- The film ran a traditional windows sequence: theaters → PVOD → pay-one → flagship streamer. Deadline contrasts this with a prior title greenlit *via* Prime Video, which went straight to the service after theatrical.
- $683M worldwide — the third-biggest global take of 2026 so far. An awards campaign is planned.
What This Actually Is
Amazon publicly demonstrating a two-track windowing doctrine. A film greenlit through the theatrical system gets the full traditional cascade — 105 days to the flagship streamer, a real theatrical run that produced $683M — while a film greenlit for the streamer skips straight to Prime. Window length isn't a fixed policy; it's a function of how the movie was greenlit and financed. For a company long ambivalent about theatrical, this is a clear statement: when Amazon MGM makes a theatrical movie, it protects the window and monetizes the full downstream chain rather than collapsing it to feed engagement.
Industry Read
1. [Deal Type] The greenlight path now predicts the window: a full ~90-105-day traditional cascade on theatrically-greenlit titles, straight-to-Prime on streamer-greenlit ones. For any project going to this buyer, which division greenlights it determines the entire distribution/monetization shape — a term to establish up front.
2. [Company] Theatrical-first at Amazon is now credible enough to price in: $683M WW plus a disciplined window proves the theatrical commitment is real for the right title, strengthening the case (and leverage) for a genuine theatrical swing there rather than assuming a streaming dump.
3. [Trend] ~105 days theatrical→flagship-streamer, with pay-one at ~90 days, is the current template for a major theatrical success at a streamer-owned studio — a windowing and downstream-participation benchmark to file.

Alamo Drafthouse Cinema Launching Distribution Label 'Alamo Exclusives' For Unreleased Film Festival Pics
· Deadline (Anthony D'Alessandro) · 2026-07-01 · https://deadline.com/2026/07/alamo-drafthouse-distribution-butthole-surfers-1236972480/ source ↗
The Facts
- Alamo Drafthouse Cinema (Sony-owned) is launching a distribution label, Alamo Exclusives, offering limited-run theatrical engagements to unreleased festival titles on its own screens.
- The stated rationale: it has become "increasingly challenged for independently financed film festival movies to find nationwide theatrical distribution."
- First title: a SXSW music documentary; tickets on sale July 21. Future programming to draw from Sundance, SXSW, Tribeca, TIFF, Cannes, Berlin and Alamo's own Fantastic Fest. Alamo will work directly with filmmakers on presentation, marketing and theatrical exposure.
What This Actually Is
Exhibitor-as-distributor vertical integration filling the indie theatrical gap the majors and streamers have vacated. Alamo owns the screens, the brand, and a cinephile audience; a distribution label lets it monetize festival orphans directly and program its theaters with exclusive content. Under Sony's ownership, it's a low-risk way for a studio-adjacent exhibitor to test grassroots distribution economics without studio-scale P&A. The backdrop is the same scarcity that strands independently financed festival films and that the specialty distributors live off.
Industry Read
1. [Company] A new theatrical outlet for festival films: filmmakers with a festival title and no distributor now have an exhibitor-run path to a real (if limited) theatrical release — a genuine addition to the distribution map for indie/doc projects.
2. [Trend] The indie-distribution gap is now structural enough that exhibitors are integrating backward to fill it. That scarcity strengthens the leverage of the specialty distributors still buying and signals the majors' retreat from indie theatrical is durable, not cyclical.
3. [Deal Type] Sony-owned exhibitor experimenting with distribution is a quiet signal on a distributor-without-a-streamer testing exhibitor-adjacent revenue — a diversification move worth filing.
